<div id="review_194248613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194248613" />
    <a title="Reynje" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5590906-reynje"><img alt="Reynje" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473722441p2/5590906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194248613?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reynje" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reynje" href="/user/show/5590906-reynje">Reynje</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780761374084" href="/book/show/10634910-ultraviolet">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194248613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18183081362873445982">I have fallen a little in love with this book, and honestly, I could not be more surprised.<br/> <br/>I picked up and put down Ultraviolet countless times (mostly because I have a Bowerbird-esque tendency to be drawn to shiny, coloured things), convinced that I had no intention of looking beyond the metallic cover and actually reading it. But after coming across positive review after positive review, on a whim (*cough* book buying frenzy *cough*) I bought it.<br/> <br/>Much has been made of the need to approach Ult</span>
  <span id="freeText18183081362873445982" style="display:none">I have fallen a little in love with this book, and honestly, I could not be more surprised.<br> <br>I picked up and put down Ultraviolet countless times (mostly because I have a Bowerbird-esque tendency to be drawn to shiny, coloured things), convinced that I had no intention of looking beyond the metallic cover and actually reading it. But after coming across positive review after positive review, on a whim (*cough* book buying frenzy *cough*) I bought it.<br> <br>Much has been made of the need to approach Ultraviolet unspoiled, and I agree wholeheartedly with this. But what I’m going to offer here is a review from the perspective of someone who did not read this book entirely unspoiled. Because when it comes to spoilers, I have a click-happy finger. I also have a terrible habit, that I am desperately trying to break, of flicking to the back of a book when I’m browsing in bookstores. <br> <br>When I started reading Ultraviolet, I already knew one fairly major element of the plot (not the ending, by the way, but still a critical detail). Do I wish I hadn’t spoiled myself and been able to experience this book without the taint of foreknowledge? Absolutely. Obviously, I didn’t feel the same impact at the whip-lash turn the story took as some other readers. Did this negatively affect my enjoyment of the book? Not at all. As mentioned at the outset, I fell hard for this book, and had a difficult time putting it down. I missed my tram stop because of this book. Enough said.<br> <br>The synopsis of Ultraviolet certainly has a hook to it. A sixteen year old girl is sectioned in a psychiatric institute, convinced she has killed the most popular girl at school. Yet the manner of the death, and the unique way Alison experiences the world, has her questioning her sanity, unsure where the line between her perceptions and reality lies.<br> <br><blockquote> "Everybody has a story, Alison," he said. "Everybody has things they need to hide--sometimes even from themselves."</blockquote><br> <br>I was caught up in Alison’s story within pages, and I attribute this in large part to R J Anderson’s writing, which is just beautiful. At one point I found myself reading with the book held up close to my face, as if I could breathe it in. Stylistically, the prose is rich, yet clear; lush with imagery, yet uncluttered. Alison’s condition is used powerfully to texture the story. It felt like an integral thread carefully woven into the narrative, rather than hastily tacked on or inserted at random. The way Anderson writes Alison’s perspective feels crisp and contemporary, yet at times it is also like seeing the world through a kaleidoscope. <br> <br><blockquote>”Doors flapped open and slammed shut, like the valves of a pounding heart. Footsteps splattered blue onto the fluorescent orange shriek of the alarm, and the air thickened with shouting voices..”</blockquote><br> <br>(Interestingly, going into this book knowing what I did, Anderson’s writing also lends itself to some subtle and smooth foreshadowing. Cue me smiling a little, thinking <i>‘oh, I see what you did there..’</i>)<br> <br>Another aspect of the writing I loved was the characterisation, and the way that even the secondary characters were real and vital. Anderson’s treatment of mental illness felt respectful and well-handled, using Alison’s own perceptions as a lens through which to view common misconceptions. Additionally, one of the characters I came to love began as possibly the most unlikely candidate, (and I can’t write that in a less convoluted manner without spoiling – suffice to say that enjoyed the way in which Anderson chose to develop her characters and subvert certain stereotypes).<br> <br>Ultraviolet does not neatly fit within the usual parameters of a given genre, which is perhaps why I ended up enjoying it as much as I did. The book largely avoids most of the tropes I was expecting, rather it blends different genre elements and becomes something of a hybrid. It is this deft sidestepping of expectations, as much as the characters themselves, that kept the story gripping for me.<br> <br>Despite the fact that I was prepared for one of the elements of the plot that the twist hinges upon, I still found Ultraviolet a compelling read. It was emotionally engaging, expressive and entertaining, which is basically my own personal trifecta of reading enjoyment (er, and a chance to abuse my alliteration privileges, apparently).<br> <br>While a companion novel is purportedly in the pipeline for 2013, Ultraviolet works beautifully as a standalone, ending on a lingering, slightly haunting, final note.<br><br>Lastly, Sebastian Faraday. All I will say here is that his name is a bit of a "Cellar Door" for me, in terms of phonaesthetics. I just wanted to keep saying it... :)<br><br><a href="http://s1130.photobucket.com/albums/m523/reynje/?action=view&amp;current=IMG_2812.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380355808i/707761._SY540_.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a>" <br> <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="18183081362873445982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_426207324" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/426207324" />
    <a title="Scarlet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3839903-scarlet"><img alt="Scarlet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556098025p2/3839903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/426207324?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scarlet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scarlet" href="/user/show/3839903-scarlet">Scarlet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11430302-ultraviolet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3839903-scarlet?shelf=put-me-to-sleep">put-me-to-sleep</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer426207324" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14717503210998950419"><u>The good thing</u>: Misleading blurb alert!! <i>Ultraviolet</i> is much more than a murder mystery.<br/><br/><u>The bad thing</u>: It still bored me to death -_-<br/><br/><br/>Before I justify my (unpopular) opinion, I have a confession to make. I was never really interested in <i>Ultraviolet</i>. Reading this book was an act of desperation. I badly needed a break from Picoult's super-sentimental preachings in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15753740.The_Storyteller' title='The Storyteller by Jodi Picoult' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Storyteller</a> and this was the only book on my Ipad that wasn't emotionally draining. So I guess this was doomed from the start.<br/><br/>---<b>Som</b></span>
  <span id="freeText14717503210998950419" style="display:none"><u>The good thing</u>: Misleading blurb alert!! <i>Ultraviolet</i> is much more than a murder mystery.<br /><br /><u>The bad thing</u>: It still bored me to death -_-<br /><br /><br />Before I justify my (unpopular) opinion, I have a confession to make. I was never really interested in <i>Ultraviolet</i>. Reading this book was an act of desperation. I badly needed a break from Picoult's super-sentimental preachings in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15753740.The_Storyteller" title="The Storyteller by Jodi Picoult" rel="nofollow noopener">The Storyteller</a> and this was the only book on my Ipad that wasn't emotionally draining. So I guess this was doomed from the start.<br /><br />---<b>Some spoilers, nothing major</b>---<br /><br /><i>Ultraviolet</i> has a fabulous concept. The MC, Allison, has <b>Synesthesia</b>, which according to Wikipedia "<i>is a neurological condition in which stimulation of one sensory or cognitive pathway leads to automatic, involuntary experiences in a second sensory or cognitive pathway.</i>"<br /><br />o_O<br /><br />In more understandable terms, all of Allison's senses are cross-wired.<br />As a result, our MC can:<br />- see/taste sounds<br />- feel/taste words<br />- see numbers as colors<br />- sense colors by touch<br />- taste feelings<br /><br />She also has a condition called <b>Tetrachromacy</b>, which according to Wikipedia... no, forget it.<br /><br />It basically means that Allison can see into the ultraviolet range so a lot of the colors she sees have no names. Yet.<br /><br />Both disorders exist in reality. However, Allison's condition in the book is <b>very, very exaggerated</b> (like most things in YA). It's like super-synesthesia or something. She can even <i>see</i> heartbeats, taste the wind, hear stars, blah blah.<br /><br />Here's what I don't understand:<br /><b>How did she not end up in a psych ward sooner??</b><br /><br />The kind of sensory overload this book describes.. it's too much. She lived like this for 16 years and nobody had a clue?? How did she not have a major mental breakdown as a child??<br /><br />That's not the reason for my low rating, by the way. That's just a random question that popped up in my head.<br /><br />My reasons are:<br /><br />- Allison. She annoyed me. For someone with such gifted sensory perception, her narration felt painfully monotonous to me.<br /><br />- The writing. Too simplistic.<br /><br />- Nearly 80% of the story takes place in a psych ward, which is not a setting I like very much. <br /><br />- The Ending. I mean, SERIOUSLY?? It was absolutely lame. It hit me like a paint-ball in the face - sudden, painful, unpleasant.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />Allison's therapist turns out to be an alien.<br />Her classmate Tori, who 'disintegrated' according to the blurb was just sucked into another dimension.<br />Oh, and Tori is an alien too.<br />But they're not aliens, they are actually humans who come from another colony.<br />And of course, the human Allison is in love with her human-yet-alien therapist.<br />WTF.<br /><br />Ever since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16101128.The_5th_Wave__The_Fifth_Wave___1_" title="The 5th Wave (The Fifth Wave, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">The 5th Wave</a>, the word 'alien' in YA makes me see red (no synesthesia-related pun intended). So maybe it's me, not the book.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Sorry if this review is disjointed, I'm <s>mentally</s> exhausted.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14717503210998950419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_166342465" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166342465" />
    <a title="Lucy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3813315-lucy"><img alt="Lucy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307662921p2/3813315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166342465?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucy" href="/user/show/3813315-lucy">Lucy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780761374084" href="/book/show/10634910-ultraviolet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813315-lucy?shelf=parents-are-evil">parents-are-evil</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813315-lucy?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813315-lucy?shelf=weird-world-building">weird-world-building</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813315-lucy?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813315-lucy?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3813315-lucy?shelf=better-than-i-expected">better-than-i-expected</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166342465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14342363869338627153">Sixteen, almost seventeen, year old Alison wakes up in a psych ward after a mental breakdown during which she claimed over and over again she killed one of the most popular girls in her high school. From page one, Alison attempts to convince herself that her break with reality was a temporary thing, even though she grew up believing she might be insane. Alison has always experienced synthesia although she knows nothing about it beyond her personal experiences. Her mother thinks she's schizophren</span>
  <span id="freeText14342363869338627153" style="display:none">Sixteen, almost seventeen, year old Alison wakes up in a psych ward after a mental breakdown during which she claimed over and over again she killed one of the most popular girls in her high school. From page one, Alison attempts to convince herself that her break with reality was a temporary thing, even though she grew up believing she might be insane. Alison has always experienced synthesia although she knows nothing about it beyond her personal experiences. Her mother thinks she's schizophrenic or something because of the synthesia and has kept her at arm's length ever since she found out.<br /><br />I can't even begin to tell you how annoying I found Alison's lack of knowledge about synthesia. It was a huge stumbling block for me while I was reading the book. I can't imagine someone thinking they had a mental illness and not researching it thoroughly to help them figure out whether or not they are crazy and, more importantly, how the disease would progress so they could get ahead of it. Yes, I come from a generation of WebMD, but Alison is supposed to be years younger than me. It should practically be in her DNA. When in doubt google! (I mean, come on, every YA paranormal romance heroine googles what the object of her affection is, but now people aren't googling legitimate conditions?) I don't really care that she doesn't like computers. It's not really a valid excuse given how much she fretted about her insanity. I said this in the comments, but I legitimately hit google if I think my <i>pee</i> smells funny... If I thought I had a ticking time bomb in my brain I'd probably be able to write books on mental illness or brain tumors etc etc.<br /><br />Alison spends a lot of time scheming to get out of the hospital and ignoring her prescribed anti-psychotics. Her memories of Tori, the popular missing girl she claimed to kill, begin to surface. She thinks she <i>disintegrated</i> Tori, which nicely brought back around the idea that she might actually be insane for those of us who knew what synthesia was from page one. <br /><br />I enjoyed her journey of self-discovery despite thinking she should have used the power of google. Some people might find the descriptions of what she experiences a little purple, but if there was ever an excuse to get a little purple in your descriptions writing about synthesia was it. The author did an excellent job of helping the audience peek into Alison's world, with vivid descriptions of taste, smell, sight, and hearing.<br /><br />The person who helps Alison discover what synthesia is happens to be an adult <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[pretending to be <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> researcher who specializes in it. The tentative romance between Sebastian and Alison... did not make me happy. I'm beating a dead horse here. Hell, I'm really beating the contents of a dead zoo really, but I'm never happy when YA fiction portrays a relationship between a teenage girl and a significantly (even he acknowledges he's too old for her) older man in a positive light. I'm even less happy that he was supposed to be staff at her psychiatric ward. She opened up to him more than she ever did to her therapist and the way the romance developed from there always felt more than a little icky to me because of it.<br /><br />I also didn't whole-heartedly buy... <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[that the hospital and doctor treating her failed to tell her parents about the fake researcher who crept in to be close to their underage daughter. Alison has been deemed unfit for making these choices for herself and they leave themselves open to huge potential lawsuits by not fully informing her family of the incident. Of course, they also let her leave the hospital a few days later on top of that. Author made things too easy for herself at the cost of believability. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The plot twists were, however, really great. The story took an interesting direction instead of treading in genre clichés, for that alone it deserved four stars. I will happily read the next book whenever it comes out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14342363869338627153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_174163901" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/174163901" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/174163901?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling">Wendy Darling</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=for-review">for-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer174163901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9317135078836960612">3 1/2 stars<br/><br/>Raise your hand if you’ve ever read a passage like this in a young adult novel:<br/><br/><i>”Comet trails of indigo and violet streaked through my inner vision, and electricity sparked all over my skin. Our breaths mingled, quick and shallow, while my lips melted open and the blood pounded through me in a pleasure so intense it was only a shade away from pain.”</i><br/><br/>Now raise your hand if you’ve ever given yourself eye strain from frequent eye rolling at passages like that. Guess who has two hands raise</span>
  <span id="freeText9317135078836960612" style="display:none">3 1/2 stars<br /><br />Raise your hand if you’ve ever read a passage like this in a young adult novel:<br /><br /><i>”Comet trails of indigo and violet streaked through my inner vision, and electricity sparked all over my skin. Our breaths mingled, quick and shallow, while my lips melted open and the blood pounded through me in a pleasure so intense it was only a shade away from pain.”</i><br /><br />Now raise your hand if you’ve ever given yourself eye strain from frequent eye rolling at passages like that. Guess who has two hands raised right now and normally hates overly exaggerated and frequent descriptions of completely unrealistic physical reactions (and can apparently type without hands)? ME. So you can imagine how surprised I was to discover that this book has the ultimate justification for exactly that: synesthesia. That’s right! So when this girl describes mild lust induced electrocution, and crazy color hallucinations, she really means it!<br /><br />Fortunately, the author uses a very controlled hand when adding in these descriptive passages; they aren’t overdone, and they are so much more intense because of that. We get pulled into Alison’s unstable mind and hazy recollections as she tries to unravel the events of a traumatic afternoon when she may have murdered the most popular girl in her school. I won’t say much more than that, because the best part of reading the beginning of this book is not knowing.<br /><br />Although I loved the writing, this was not a perfect read for me. The main character is so wrapped up in her own mind and confused senses that she inaccurately judges most of the people around her (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[despite being able to taste lies later in the book <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). She also has had a lifetime of hiding and denying her condition, which goes pretty far in explaining her actions, but doesn’t make it any less frustrating to witness her shoot herself in the foot time and time again. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Did anyone else wish that Tori had been the main character? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Many of the characters seem to take dramatic 180 degree turns throughout the book. Some of this can be explained by the coloring of events by Alison’s perception, but I felt like I had a bit of whiplash by the end. <br /><br />I also didn’t feel any chemistry between Alison and the love interest. In the beginning, I even felt a little icky about the whole thing, because they meet and interact in a scenario where romance would be highly inappropriate (I know I am being extremely vague, but I am trying my hardest not to spoil anything).<br /><br />I did see the twist coming from pretty early on, but it’s a refreshing change in the “paranormal” genre. I also don’t think that everything has been revealed quite yet. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I mean, there <i>has</i> to be something going on with her super extra special senses, right? I’m guessing she’s part alien. Who’s with me? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>Perfect Musical Pairing</u><br /><br />The Red Paintings – <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=IpFt3dFHm9w" rel="nofollow noopener">Walls</a><br /><br />I’ll admit that I had never heard of these guys before, but I was reading some things about synesthesia on the interweb and I discovered that their lead singer is a synesthete! I love the string instruments and creative lyrics in this song, which seems to be about holding on to the good memories from a relationship, even though it is impossible to be together.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9317135078836960612" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_171436755" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171436755" />
    <a title="Jo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2467655-jo"><img alt="Jo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596119990p2/2467655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/171436755?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo" href="/user/show/2467655-jo">Jo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171436755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5341443411823220394"><b>Initial Final Page Thoughts.</b><br/>Enjoyable… but I kind of wish we could’ve read t’other story mentioned at the beginning with the girl with the odd-shaped birthmark. No offense Alison, you’re cool and all but… yeah. Also, can you say ‘sequel’? I’d be OK with that, I think.<br/><br/><b>High Points.</b><br/>What an opening! Highly visual writing that did not provoke a single eye-roll (OK, there was a little towards the end but I’ll discuss that later). A strong heroine who only gets a bit annoying towards the end. Realistic</span>
  <span id="freeText5341443411823220394" style="display:none"><b>Initial Final Page Thoughts.</b><br />Enjoyable… but I kind of wish we could’ve read t’other story mentioned at the beginning with the girl with the odd-shaped birthmark. No offense Alison, you’re cool and all but… yeah. Also, can you say ‘sequel’? I’d be OK with that, I think.<br /><br /><b>High Points.</b><br />What an opening! Highly visual writing that did not provoke a single eye-roll (OK, there was a little towards the end but I’ll discuss that later). A strong heroine who only gets a bit annoying towards the end. Realistic portrayal of people’s reaction to ‘crazy’ people. Flashbacks. Mystery and intrigue. Synaesthesia. An interesting venture into the paranormal, even though it wasn’t executed as clean as I would’ve liked (and why wasn’t that bit longer?) , I was interested and impressed that we didn’t get a vamp-attack. (I admit when I read the reviews saying there was an ‘unexpected twist’ I was worried it was suddenly going to take a turn for the vamp). <br /><br /><b>Low Points.</b><br /><i>Insta-love</i>... *shudder* my arch-nemesis. Yaccck. Well, OK… maybe I’m being unfair because it actually wasn’t insta-love, but they hardly mentioned any cute falling-in-love moments so I found it really difficult to believe their relationship. It was quite bland... but then it was too much and hit you with the subtlety of a train. Also.. I'm a bit of a science geek so I really wanted to find out more about her condition from a scientific p.o.v and not just confused and hazy descriptions! Looks like Wikipedia will <i>have</i> to do! <br /><br /><b>Hero. </b><br />Alison was a funny one. I really liked her at first and I thought her narration with the way she sees the world was both beautiful and terrifying. In the first few chapters she really captured the frustration of having a condition that you couldn’t explain and feeling alienated from everyone because of it, but as the story went on and she learnt more about her condition I felt like she’d run out of excuses. And she just got a bit annoying, self-indulgent and seemed to really lose her spark, which was a shame because she really had potential.<br />Maybe in the sequel she’ll sort herself out. <br /><br /><b>Love Interest.</b><br />Kind of covered this in the low point. I could’ve lived without this aspect of the book but I didn’t hate it completely, mostly because Sebastian sounded pretty cute, but there was zilch tummy-flipping swoon moments. But it did provoke a few eye-rolls and a few cynical mutterings under my breath especially at the end (you’ll know which bit if you’ve read it). Ack. <br /><br /><b>Theme Tune.</b> <br /><br /><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PULdPep_xfs" rel="nofollow noopener">Starry Eyed- Ellie Goulding. </a><br />The thing I loved the most about this book was the descriptions of how Alison saw the world with her condition. I imagined it to be like a comic book, full of bright colours and fireworks. A pretty cool super-power that I don’t think she took advantage of. Anyway, a bit that I really liked was the part where Alison was younger and she was watching her mother do the dishes and, when she heard the clink of the cutlery together, she saw stars. Gettit? <br /><br /><br /><b>Angst Scale.</b><br />6/10. All six points are being awarded to the beginning of the book, particularly the first 3 chapters. The idea of waking up in a mental institution with no recollection of why you’re there except that you may have killed someone?<br />That is pretty much my worst nightmare.<br />The way Alison describes her confusion and panic at her situation was really frightening, vivid and such excellent writing from Anderson that I stayed up way past my bed time to finish it! <br />There is also a few points added for the condescending treatment of Alison at the hands of her mother and her best friend (they’re the worst) which was, unfortunately, all too easy to imagine because people are still ignorant of mental conditions and old taboos are still very much in play here. <br />I also want to add about 20 points for the ‘love-angst’ but I refuse to encourage that kind of behaviour… so you’re only getting 6. <br /><br /><b>Recommended For.</b><br />People who like their YA a bit darker. People who like extremely visual, but really great, writing. People who don’t mind when their heroines pick up and move to planet ‘Look at me! Look at me!’ (Props to anyone who gets that reference from the <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=w6XGUhzfutc" rel="nofollow noopener">best teen flick in the world </a>). <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[People who like paranormal fiction that is out of this world. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> People who are interested in science and mental conditions. People who always think the bitchy characters are more interesting and should be the main character. People who get inappropriate crushes on authority figures.<br /><br /><br />You can also read the review for this book and others along with a whole lot of other exciting stuff on my blog <a href="http://weartheoldcoat.blogspot.com" rel="nofollow noopener">here.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5341443411823220394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_171429026" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171429026" />
    <a title="Jasprit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4841793-jasprit"><img alt="Jasprit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456315622p2/4841793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/171429026?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jasprit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jasprit" href="/user/show/4841793-jasprit">Jasprit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4841793-jasprit?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4841793-jasprit?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4841793-jasprit?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4841793-jasprit?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4841793-jasprit?shelf=out-2011">out-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171429026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10660872437512566525">4.5 stars<br/><br/>Ultraviolet reminded me of one of my all time favourite films Shutter Island. After finishing the book I sat there thinking to myself what has just happened?<br/><br/>In Ultraviolet we are told the story through Alison; she’s found herself in a psych unit after admitting she killed Tori a girl from school. Tori, hasn’t been seen since, but her blood was found at school at the spot where Alison the last person to have seen her had a confrontation with her. Alison’s mum has always believed Alison’s</span>
  <span id="freeText10660872437512566525" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br /><br />Ultraviolet reminded me of one of my all time favourite films Shutter Island. After finishing the book I sat there thinking to myself what has just happened?<br /><br />In Ultraviolet we are told the story through Alison; she’s found herself in a psych unit after admitting she killed Tori a girl from school. Tori, hasn’t been seen since, but her blood was found at school at the spot where Alison the last person to have seen her had a confrontation with her. Alison’s mum has always believed Alison’s not right, so Pine Hills is the best place for her, whereas everyone else including Alison herself just wants to discover the truth.<br /><br />Ultraviolet took me a while to warm up to, it had an unusual premise, but once we started to get down to the nitty gritty with Alison, I found myself fallen head first into an unbelieving world, but in no way I wanted to leave.<br /><br />Ultraviolet was multi-layered and complex, but Anderson was able to produce a masterly woven plot. It had aspects where I assumed I knew what was going to happen, but then was totally flummoxed by what was around the corner. I liked the way Anderson slowly took us into Alison’s life, and opened nooks and crannies that even Alison hadn’t been aware of, how we were pushed deeper into her life and emerged with so many fascinating facts.<br /><br />Alison was a character who I enjoyed being with, she couldn’t understand what was going on either, whether she really killed Tori, so I liked going through this journey with her, slowly unravelling the mystery surrounding herself and what really happened. It was like she was wearing blinkers her entire life and she couldn’t really see what was really happening. Until that one person came into her life who had that faith in her that she needed to give her the confidence to believe in herself. This one character I’m just a loss for words when describing him, it could probably be best summed up by Alison<br /><br /><i>“I was terrified of losing..., a soul mate so perfectly made for me that even now I was half afraid I’d invented him”.</i><br /><br />And Alison nails it with this quote what Ultraviolet is really about. We are often led to believe so much, but how honestly can we say that it’s true? It’s like me and my best friend can see the same fight but have totally different takes on it afterwards. My sense of reality can be quite different to another’s. In essence Anderson has been able to play on this in an unbelievable way with Ultraviolet.<br /><br />I went into Ultraviolet with one story, but left with so many more. With Ultraviolet just be prepared for the unexpected.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10660872437512566525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588746288p2/10326608.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2157484829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16758102231508877658">Alison is in a mental institution following a breakdown where she (somehow) disintegrated another sixteen-year old girl. The police and the doctors want to know what happened to Tori, where she has disappeared to and why Alison had Tori's blood on her hands. Alison struggles with her doctors, her medication and her own history of mental health issues and it all comes to a head when a new doctor visits the hospital and professes to believe Alison's bizarre story.<br/><br/>This story is at its strongest whi</span>
  <span id="freeText16758102231508877658" style="display:none">Alison is in a mental institution following a breakdown where she (somehow) disintegrated another sixteen-year old girl. The police and the doctors want to know what happened to Tori, where she has disappeared to and why Alison had Tori's blood on her hands. Alison struggles with her doctors, her medication and her own history of mental health issues and it all comes to a head when a new doctor visits the hospital and professes to believe Alison's bizarre story.<br /><br />This story is at its strongest while dealing with Alison as a mental patient with very real doubts about her sanity and an even greater distrust of her doctors. Her choices while in the institution come under a sharp focus, as does her relationship with her family. Not to say that the science fiction story that kicks everything off and dominates the second half of the book is bad: it's not. It's just that the mental health elements of the book are the strongest.<br /><br />Fairly good YA SF with a unique and vulnerable protagonist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16758102231508877658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_158576066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158576066" />
    <a title="Nic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2813423-nic"><img alt="Nic" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/158576066?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nic" href="/user/show/2813423-nic">Nic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2813423-nic?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2813423-nic?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2813423-nic?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2813423-nic?shelf=out-of-this-world">out-of-this-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2813423-nic?shelf=review-copy">review-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer158576066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer565867557012143698"><b>Favourite Quote:</b> <i>"I might not be ready to pour out my feelings to the world, but I’d had enough of trying to ignore them."</i><br/><br/>Wow! Ultraviolet is an incredibly original and hypnotic read. I just loved how refreshingly different it was from the plot to the characters to even the style of writing. Everything was unique and it that made for such a standout read.<br/><br/>Ultraviolet is the story of Alison who is placed in a psychiatric unit after complete breakdown. She is also the main suspect in the murder cas</span>
  <span id="freeText565867557012143698" style="display:none"><b>Favourite Quote:</b> <i>"I might not be ready to pour out my feelings to the world, but I’d had enough of trying to ignore them."</i><br /><br />Wow! Ultraviolet is an incredibly original and hypnotic read. I just loved how refreshingly different it was from the plot to the characters to even the style of writing. Everything was unique and it that made for such a standout read.<br /><br />Ultraviolet is the story of Alison who is placed in a psychiatric unit after complete breakdown. She is also the main suspect in the murder case of the most popular girl at school Tori. But nobody knows what happened including Alison as there is no body. All Alison can remember is one moment she was arguing with Tori then next thing she had disintegrated into thin air. Is Alison going crazy? Or there an extraordinary answer for it all?<br /><br />The plot pulls you in from the first page and you are completely hooked until the end. It is filled with some very delicious unexpected twists. I really thought I knew what was going on but I had no idea. I love that Ultraviolet was not what I expected because it ended up being so much more.<br /><br />R.J. Anderson writing is alluring and vividly descriptive especially when it came to describing Alison condition of synesthesia - tasting words, how each letter has a personality and a colour and in general just seeing so much more than the rest of us. It was definitely interesting element to the story and made Alison an extraordinary character.<br /><br /><i>"Seconds shaded into minutes, and the butterflies in my stomach had turned into biting horseflies, when a candy-pink squeal of delight rippled down the corridor towards me."</i><br /><br />Alison had an utterly convincing voice. She had to deal with some hard situations - locked in a mental facility, possibly killing someone, thinking she might be going crazy but her reactions were always believable. Now Faraday in a unforgettable character. Smart, kind and surprising. I only wish we had gotten more of him because I really enjoyed his presence in the story.<br /><br /><i>"Dark chocolate, poured over velvet: that was how his voice tasted. I wanted him to follow me around and narrate the rest of my life."</i> <br /><br />As for the romance it definitely didn't feel like the typical young adult romantic situation. It was that something special and yet it didn't take the focus away from the amazing plot.<br /><br />Overall, Ultraviolet is a stunning out of the ordinary read. I definitely recommend it to people looking for something different but yet still an exceptional story. Now I just need to get my hands on more of R.J. Anderson's books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="565867557012143698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_173915865" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/173915865" />
    <a title="Limonessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4147373-limonessa"><img alt="Limonessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424025988p2/4147373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/173915865?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Limonessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Limonessa" href="/user/show/4147373-limonessa">Limonessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4147373-limonessa?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4147373-limonessa?shelf=ya-lit">ya-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer173915865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6955819842813604749">In every novel worth its name there is an abundance of figures of speech such as metaphor, simile, oxymoron. They are part of the texture of the narration and are employed to enforce the first rule of Writing 101, which is to show and not to tell.<br/><br/>I remember quite distinctly having studied rhetorical figures during my high school years in Italian classes and I knew synesthesia from such classes. I still remember one example quite distinctly taken from a poem by Pascoli: “un pigolio di stelle” – a</span>
  <span id="freeText6955819842813604749" style="display:none">In every novel worth its name there is an abundance of figures of speech such as metaphor, simile, oxymoron. They are part of the texture of the narration and are employed to enforce the first rule of Writing 101, which is to show and not to tell.<br /><br />I remember quite distinctly having studied rhetorical figures during my high school years in Italian classes and I knew synesthesia from such classes. I still remember one example quite distinctly taken from a poem by Pascoli: “un pigolio di stelle” – a chirping of stars. In a synesthesia you juxtapose two terms which belong to two different sensorial plains, ex. “a bitter smell”.<br />I never, ever knew it could be – actually, the figure of speech probably comes from – a neurological condition.<br />In this book, at least in the first part, such figures of speech not only abound, but are the foundation on which the whole novel is based. <br /><br />Alison wakes up in a mental ward. She barely remembers what led her to being committed, except that she’s had a mental breakdown and that she probably has murdered her high school nemesis by…. disintegrating her. <br />With the help of Faraday, a fascinating therapist, she will discover the truth behind her unlikely condition and what really happened the night she does not remember.<br /><br />This book is, without a doubt, the weirdest I have read in a long time. It starts off as one genre and then, three quarters into it, completely turns into something else. And when I say weird I mean that it’s like you’re reading a scene from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6185.Wuthering_Heights" title="Wuthering Heights by Emily Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Wuthering Heights</a> and Martin McFly on the Delorean comes stealing the scene. That kind of weird. <br />But I’d rather not talk about the plot or the characters any more because it’s really better if you go into this book by not knowing anymore than what I already said.<br /><br />I am still not sure I completely appreciated the plot twist and probably never will make up my mind. I really, really liked the first part and for me it was a 5 star all the way but all of a sudden… the plot takes a weird turn, the register drops, the figures of speech disappear and… you might or might not like it. It still deserves a solid four stars in my opinion, the writing is extremely good, well worth reading this original story but I have to confess that to me it kind of stopped making sense from a certain point on. <br />In fact, the question that was in my mind while reading and that honestly I still haven’t completely discarded is: <b>is Alison an unreliable narrator?</b><br />To me this question is still open, and since I am pretty sure there is going to be a – or maybe multiple – sequel/s to this YA book which certainly deserves a special attention and that deviates from “the usual”, I guess we’ll just have to wait and see what happens next.<br /><br />Definitely recommended, the writing is exceptional.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6955819842813604749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_152250994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/152250994" />
    <a title="Sandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4515165-sandy"><img alt="Sandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320017147p2/4515165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/152250994?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandy" href="/user/show/4515165-sandy">Sandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152250994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13332171216287428989"><b>4.5 out of 5 stars</b><br/><br/>So unexpected in such a good way. It's impossible to talk about how amazing this book is without delving into spoiler territory, so I'm keeping my recommendation vague and short: <b>READ THIS.</b><br/><br/>You start with that stellar hook (kudos to R.J. for captivating me from chapter zero) and you think you know what type of book <i>Ultraviolet</i> is going to be, but then it completely twists and morphs through different genres to become an entirely different book. <b>And R. J. Anderson makes it WORK.</b><br/><br/>(</span>
  <span id="freeText13332171216287428989" style="display:none"><b>4.5 out of 5 stars</b><br /><br />So unexpected in such a good way. It's impossible to talk about how amazing this book is without delving into spoiler territory, so I'm keeping my recommendation vague and short: <b>READ THIS.</b><br /><br />You start with that stellar hook (kudos to R.J. for captivating me from chapter zero) and you think you know what type of book <i>Ultraviolet</i> is going to be, but then it completely twists and morphs through different genres to become an entirely different book. <b>And R. J. Anderson makes it WORK.</b><br /><br />(Can I just say how refreshing it is to have a scientific explanation for a character's special abilities? None of this <i>you have these powers 'cuz you just do.</i> Nope. There is a brilliant explanation for everything that happens in this book, and I. LOVED. IT.)<br /><br />I recommend going into this one blind. I didn't even read the blurb--I just started reading--and that enhanced the experience of immersing myself in this absorbing, mind-bending novel.<br /><br />Lastly, that GORGEOUS writing. There is some stunning imagery in <i>Ultraviolet</i> that touches all of the senses. I was swept away by the unique, stirring descriptions for the way Alison senses the world. It's unlike anything I've read this year. Where did R.J. come up with so many different ways to describe the mundane things we see and hear every day? Seriously, that's some beautiful writing.<br /><br />Avoid spoilers like mad and go read this book.</span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780761374084" href="/book/show/10634910-ultraviolet">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1233686-megan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1233686-megan?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166345629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7053660326581441251">Wow. Fortunately I had an entire day to devote to <i>Ultraviolet</i> because I was completely hooked. If you are reading this review you must have read the synopsis and know that it begins with sixteen (almost seventeen) year old Alison waking up in a psychiatric facility. Alison initially has no recollection of what placed her in the hospital, and in fact does not even have a history of mental illness. <br/><br/>Author R.J.Anderson does an amazing, simply amazing job of setting the stage for "is Alison crazy or</span>
  <span id="freeText7053660326581441251" style="display:none">Wow. Fortunately I had an entire day to devote to <i>Ultraviolet</i> because I was completely hooked. If you are reading this review you must have read the synopsis and know that it begins with sixteen (almost seventeen) year old Alison waking up in a psychiatric facility. Alison initially has no recollection of what placed her in the hospital, and in fact does not even have a history of mental illness. <br /><br />Author R.J.Anderson does an amazing, simply amazing job of setting the stage for "is Alison crazy or isn't she?" The fine line between mental illness and paranormal activity is one of my all time favorite book subjects and it is done so very well here. And of course there is the <i>twist</i> in this book...holy fuck! I had a vague idea, but never in a million years thought Anderson would go there. But she did, and it worked! However, is it only the mystery plot line which makes this book so great? Ah...yes and no. <br /><br />On one hand, there is the surprise and uniqueness factor. <i>Ultraviolet</i> is well written and for sure stands alone amongst so many contemporary YA's. But on the other hand, once we arrived at the, er... twisty aspect of the novel, the writing was slightly rushed and not quite up to the same standard it had been. Granted, this portion of the novel was heavy on the action, so some rushing is expected but somehow it just felt too out of tune with the rest of the writing. <br /><br />Finally, I wish the supporting characters had been more fully developed. Although we do learn so much about Alison's family, her BFF Melissa, and the other patients at the psychiatric facility I can't help but feel we see them at a distance. That is, rather than reading a well developed novel, it feels as if we are seeing them solely through Alison's eyes. This is a hard point for me to critique though, because Alison is shy, aloof, and frequently lost in her own world. So it is expected that she does not provide, or imagine in depth characterizations of these people. However, as the reader of this novel, I really wanted to know them all a little more intimately. <br /><br />For those who don't mind excessive descriptions and imagery and are looking for something intense and different, <i>Ultraviolet</i> is a book I whole-hardheartedly recommend. It is mysterious, beautifully written, and took me on one heck of an emotional journey. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7053660326581441251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2441893046" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16701680240850244019">Usually, I like going into books blind, however 'Ultraviolet' was testament for why that's sometimes not a good idea. <br/><br/>R.J.Anderson is the author of one of my all-time favourite series, Knife (known as Faery Rebels in the US), and I'd seen 'Ultraviolet' advertised in the back of my copy of Arrow, so I've been aware of this books existence for a long time. But back then I wasn't really into thrillers, and it was only after hearing that the main character in the companion novel, 'QuickSilver', iden</span>
  <span id="freeText16701680240850244019" style="display:none">Usually, I like going into books blind, however 'Ultraviolet' was testament for why that's sometimes not a good idea. <br /><br />R.J.Anderson is the author of one of my all-time favourite series, Knife (known as Faery Rebels in the US), and I'd seen 'Ultraviolet' advertised in the back of my copy of Arrow, so I've been aware of this books existence for a long time. But back then I wasn't really into thrillers, and it was only after hearing that the main character in the companion novel, 'QuickSilver', identifies as asexual that I thought I should start the series. <br /><br />The beginning seemed really promising - the writing was as good as I remembered - but then it started to drag in the middle. It's not the first book I've read about a synesthete, and not the first book I've read set in a mental facility either, so maybe if it had been the first on either of those accounts I would have found it more entertaining. The reason I say I wish I'd known more about it was mostly the shock sci-fi twist that occurs 75% through. <br /><br />For an otherwise contemporary, what I believed to be thriller, this required too much suspension of disbelief, and if it wasn't for the hope of an ace protagonist in the companion, I probably would've DNF'd. Although, the ace girl is an alien so...so much for representation. But I'm holding my tongue, because I haven't read book two yet, and I need to go in with an open mind! </span>

<div id="review_2156651934" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2156651934" />
    <a title="Veronique" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12371602-veronique"><img alt="Veronique" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590509341p2/12371602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2156651934?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronique" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronique" href="/user/show/12371602-veronique">Veronique</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=r2017">r2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=stars-4-0">stars-4-0</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2156651934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6504540984289589235"><i>“Everybody has a story, Alison," he said. “Everybody has things they need to hide--sometimes even from themselves.” </i><br/><br/>To set a novel mostly in a mental institute was something I just couldn’t miss. And Anderson pulls it off brilliantly. Here we have the experience of a teenager finding herself in such a place, trying to deal with her situation. Why is she here? What happened? How is she going to adapt to this restricted and indeed disturbing ‘asylum’? Through her own experience and what she sees o</span>
  <span id="freeText6504540984289589235" style="display:none"><i>“Everybody has a story, Alison," he said. “Everybody has things they need to hide--sometimes even from themselves.” </i><br /><br />To set a novel mostly in a mental institute was something I just couldn’t miss. And Anderson pulls it off brilliantly. Here we have the experience of a teenager finding herself in such a place, trying to deal with her situation. Why is she here? What happened? How is she going to adapt to this restricted and indeed disturbing ‘asylum’? Through her own experience and what she sees of others, the author paints a raw picture of how people with mental illnesses are treated, chipping little by little at all the taboos and unsavoury aspects, shining a torch at them, all to make us understand that nothing is as bad as imagined. Yes, there is pain and injustice and violence and anger and plenty of other emotions, but that is not the whole thing, and our perceptions can and often are totally askew. <br /><br />The many characters and relationships offered here are complex, and hidden in a way. The author does choose some very interesting paths that don’t become visible until the end, playing with our understanding of the people around us, and especially ourselves. Then, Anderson adds the synaesthesia card, and again I’m beyond sold. I’ve been fascinated by this for quite a while now, wondering how it would be to taste or see sounds. Sadly, it is only too easy to understand how people could have misunderstood this and seen only schizophrenia. <br /><br />The story does dip into science fiction, which wasn’t needed - the book would have been perfect without it - but I can see the appeal of it too. Under the umbrella of this genre, the author could probably reach a wider audience :O)</span>

<div id="review_176280030" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176280030" />
    <a title="Jessie  (Ageless Pages Reviews)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews"><img alt="Jessie  (Ageless Pages Reviews)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603380977p2/4863162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176280030?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessie  (Ageless Pages Reviews)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessie  (Ageless Pages Reviews)" href="/user/show/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews">Jessie  (Ageless Pages Reviews)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=supernatural-fiction">supernatural-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=owned-copy">owned-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=netgalley-arc">netgalley-arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=five-stars-for-you-glen-coco">five-stars-for-you-glen-coco</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=curious-and-curiouser">curious-and-curiouser</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=ya-fiction">ya-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=inventive-and-original">inventive-and-original</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=2011-reads">2011-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863162-jessie-ageless-pages-reviews?shelf=reviews-2011">reviews-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176280030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13713119833136141531"><b>Read <a href='http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/2011/06/review-ultraviolet-by-rj-anderson.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>This Review</a> & More Like It <a href='http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>On My Blog</a>!</b><br/><br/>This uniquely imaginative and intelligent novel was a terrifically melded blend of mystery, science fiction, fantasy and young-adult genres. Told through the eyes and life of Alison Jeffries, a seventeen year old girl, Alison is both a very unreliable narrator and a hugely sympathetic character. R.J. Anderson truly achieved the voice, and attitude of a sullen, hurting young woman. Alison is a living, breathing, three-dimensional character filled with fl</span>
  <span id="freeText13713119833136141531" style="display:none"><b>Read <a href="http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/2011/06/review-ultraviolet-by-rj-anderson.html" rel="nofollow noopener">This Review</a> &amp; More Like It <a href="http://agelesspagesreviews.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">On My Blog</a>!</b><br /><br />This uniquely imaginative and intelligent novel was a terrifically melded blend of mystery, science fiction, fantasy and young-adult genres. Told through the eyes and life of Alison Jeffries, a seventeen year old girl, Alison is both a very unreliable narrator and a hugely sympathetic character. R.J. Anderson truly achieved the voice, and attitude of a sullen, hurting young woman. Alison is a living, breathing, three-dimensional character filled with flaws, virtues and humanity. As Alison, the narrative is filled with passion and viable emotions and thoughts. Her wry (and often self-deprecating) humor were dead on the mark for a teenager who has been taught to be ashamed of all she is and can do.<br /><br />This is a novel that was crafted with delicacy and much planning. It is laden with clues, subtle hints, and hidden meanings deep in the imagery-heavy, sensory-rich prose. I do not feel that revealing Alison has synesthesia as a spoiler -- it's out mentioned in the in the ads. Words, numbers, sounds all have personalities, colors, smells thanks to her possessing five different kinds of the phenomenon. Alison, while driving in a car states, "[...]I wanted to hear the landscape, taste its contours, and smell its hues," as only she can. Her amazingly vivid condition fits the lush style of the writing well: it's as close as the reader will ever get to experience life the way Alison does. I was so interested in this very real condition that I researched it online and I am beyond impressed with the depth of research and history Anderson went to in order for this story to work on the levels it does.<br /><br />I enjoyed the fresh scenery: I've not read <s>any</s> hardly any novels set in Canada and the change of scene was a nice harbinger of the individuality to follow. The atmosphere of the story was completely enveloping. Even necessary the parts of the novel (for example Part One was <i>The Scent of Yesterday</i>, chapters are titled <b>Zero(Is Translucent)</b>, <b>One (Is Gray)</b>, <b>Ten (Is Vulernable)</b>, etc.) are subtle reminders that hearken back to the most fascinating aspect of the novel: Alison's abilities. The first part of the novel focuses much more on the mystery aspect of Alison's story: what exactly did happen to Tori, and was Alison in any way responsible for Tori's death/disappearance. Part one was intense and impossible to extract myself from as the pieces were slowly revealed. The more Alison pulls herself and her memory together, details about the mysterious event are doled out like nuggets of gold. The true events of the mystery are parceled out so stingily, for the first hundred pages I genuinely could not decide if I believed Alison was sane or not. Now that's an unreliable narrator: one who does not even trust herself or her recollections. Part two <i>(Present Sense)</i> suffers just a bit from a rushed, slightly uneven tempo, but happily the problem was short-lived: part three (<i>Touching Tomorrow</i>) managed to be well-rounded, nicely executed and soulful conclusion to a delightfully surprising novel. The ending is more bitter than sweet, but is entirely appropriate and fitting for Alison's journey. There are a few opportunities and plot-lines left open for exploration in a possible sequel, one I can only hope is written soon.<br /><br />his is definitely more of a plot-driven novel. The rush to figure out what happened to Alison, to Tori, to be placed under her own cognizance, moves the characters more than romance or friendship. There was a deft touch with the tension in the novel: it builds slowly, marginally and then ratchets up to 11 in the final scenes. I hardly minded the plot-focus because I was entirely caught up in the uniquely creative language and prose. Descriptions like "his hair was the color of a thunderstorm reflected in a mud puddle" will win me over any day of the week., especially if interpersonal interaction is not a strong point of the author's. And, to be honest, some of the love/emotional scenes were a bit too saccharinely sweet for my taste. However, I do love creative, innovative writers than can make their words and ideas pop: R.J. Anderson is definitely one such author.<br /><br />This is a novel that more than lives up to its advertising byline: Everything You Know Is Wrong. But you'll only know why if you read this novel. Its unique premise, gorgeous prose, full of quotes to love, and more than helluva twist more than recommend it.<br /><br /><blockquote>"I heard the universe as an oratorio sung by a master choir accompanied by the orchestra of the planets and the percussion of satellites and moons. The aria they performed was a song to break the heart, full of tragic dissonance and deferred hope, and yet somewhere beneath it all was a piercing refrain of glory, glory, glory. And I sensed that not only the grand movements of the cosmos, but everything that had happened in my life, was a part of that song. Even the hurts that seemed most senseless, the mistakes I would have done anything to erase--nothing could make those things good, but good could still come out of them all the same, and in the end the oratorio would be no less beautiful for it."</blockquote><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13713119833136141531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
